Near this ravine, I was tracking some bird movement in the foliage. Suddenly, I spied a PALMER'S CHIPMUNK  (Tamias palmeri ) scurry over the scree and continue further down into the steep ravine. Although it was a brief view, I did have a good look at it before it had disappeared.----Feeling immensely relieved, I went back into town for lunch. After perusing the maps, I decided against heading back to Utah and SE Idaho. Instead, I opted to head north and visit Beatty (home of the rare Amargosa Toad). From there, I would continue northward to Spokane.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS154299284	2017-05-15 17:30:19	16191	species	Hammond's Flycatcher	Empidonax hammondii			1				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003		33	USFWS_591		Desert NWR--Corn Creek (Field Station)	L168656	H	36.4387970	-115.3595660	2012-05-05	05:30:00	obsr235247	S10838974	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	420	3.219		2	1		0	1	0		Experienced an absolutely amazing migrant fallout on 5/5 at Corn Creek just north of Las Vegas.  David Donsker and I arrived at Corn Creek at first light and immediately noticed a number of kingbirds flying into the trees from the desert.  As the steady stream of birds continued, we noticed numerous warblers flying in along with the kingbirds.  The first tree we reached was loaded with Western Kingbirds, although only 10 feet tall it had at least 50 kingbirds on it perched like Christmas tree ornaments.  Each binocular field in each mulberry tree contained at least 5 Bullock's Orioles and a few more Western Kingbirds.  Groups of 5 to 10 warblers (mostly Audubon's) were in the trees, on the ground, in the grass, on fence posts - just everywhere.  We birded the oasis from 5:15 to 11 and conservatively estimated 2,000 birds of 75 species.  Counting was next to impossible as the birds were constantly flitting from tree to tree.  We returned and birded the area again from 2:30 to 5:30, and although activity was much lower, there were still hundreds of migrants, several more still flying in from the desert, and we found several species we didn't have in the morning.   Also saw 1 hybrid Eur Collared-Dove X Mourning Dove but could not determine how to enter this into e-bird.

The bird was "colorful" and had contrasty wing bars. Bill was short and dark. The structure (long wings big head with slight bushy crest), "colorful" look with contrasting gray head, small short, dark bill all identified it as a Hammond's. Very active flicking its wings and tail flying about. --<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/15889648@N00/10311705624/" title="Hammond's Flycatcher by larus fan, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm8.staticflickr.com/7356/10311705624_cb3d080e44_o.jpg" alt="Hammond's Flycatcher" /></a>

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS396796334	2020-06-18 09:51:47	16191	species	Hammond's Flycatcher	Empidonax hammondii			2				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003		33			Las Vegas--Floyd Lamb Park at Tule Springs	L139900	H	36.3249025	-115.2665940	2016-04-30	11:30:00	obsr221195	S29312907	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	150	2.414		2	1		0	1	0		The weather was very atypical, with rain at my place from roughly 4 AM to 10 AM, and I assume the pattern was similar at the park, along with wind on the western edges or exposed areas and 55 degree weather when we started.  There were very few people at the park except the many campers affiliated with some group.  I am not sure why but whenever they have one of these group camp events, the weather is usually miserable.  However, it seemed to be quite good for migrants and we saw many species that we would not normally expect.  Although we got there on the late side of the morning, I think it likely that the birds had not been able to feed much with the rain, but were quite voracious feeders once it had stopped for a bit.   It was a most interesting and pretty exciting outing for us.   <br />Submitted from eBird for iOS, version 1.1.5 Build 44

The bird showed a very short stubby bill, giving it a large headed appearance. The bill was strongly bi-colored. The underparts were quite yellow, perhaps in fresh plumage-I'm pretty sure they molt in the fall, so this could be expected.
